Translation Evaluation Packet

Language: Russian

Reviewer: Russian Reviewer

Date Generated: 2025-11-30

Instructions for Reviewers

Thank you for participating in this translation quality evaluation. Please follow these guidelines:

  1. Read carefully: Review the original English text, the translation, and the back-translation.
  2. Rate each criterion: Use the 1-5 scale for each quality dimension.
  3. Note critical errors: Flag any translation errors that could harm patients.
  4. Be objective: Evaluate based on accuracy, not personal preference.

Rating Scale

  • 5 - Excellent: Perfect or near-perfect quality
  • 4 - Good: Minor issues that don't affect meaning
  • 3 - Acceptable: Some issues but core meaning preserved
  • 2 - Poor: Significant issues affecting comprehension
  • 1 - Very Poor: Major errors or incomprehensible
SAFETY CRITICAL: If you find any translation that could lead to patient harm (wrong medication instructions, missing warnings, etc.), please mark it as a critical error and describe the issue in the notes section.

Evaluation Criteria Reference

CriterionDescription
Overall AccuracyHow accurately does the translation convey the original meaning?
Medical AccuracyAre medical terms and concepts translated correctly?
Cultural AppropriatenessIs the translation culturally appropriate for the target audience?
ClarityHow easy is the translation to understand for a native speaker?
Safety PreservationAre all warnings.and safety information preserved?
ActionabilityCan a patient follow the instructions based on this translation?

Evaluation Items

Item: EVAL_0013 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure ‚Äì Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart can no longer effectively pump oxygen-rich blood to the other parts of the body. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be needed. This article explains what you need to do to take care of yourself after being discharged from the hospital. When you are in the hospital You were in the hospital for treatment of heart failure. Heart failure occurs when the heart muscle is weakened or does not relax well, or both together. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. Like any pump, if the outflow from it is insufficient, fluids do not move well and stagnate where they should not. In your body this means that fluid accumulates in the lungs, abdominal cavity, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your medical team carefully regulated the amount of fluid you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also monitored and measured how much urine you passed. You may have been prescribed medicines that help the body get rid of excess fluid. You may have undergone tests to check how well your heart is working. What to expect at home Your energy will gradually return. At first, when you return home, you may need help taking care of yourself. You may feel sad or depressed. All of this is normal. Self-monitoring at home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale right after waking up ‚Äì before eating, but after using the toilet. Make sure that each time you weigh yourself in similar clothing. Record your weight every day in a chart to track it. During the day ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Is it becoming more difficult for me to breathe when doing my usual daily activities? Have my clothes or shoes become tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you have new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something unusual or try a new ...

Item: EVAL_0014 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model A

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure ‚Äî Hospital Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart can no longer effectively pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body. With severe symptoms, hospitalization may be required. This article describes what you need to do to care for yourself after discharge from the hospital. When you were in the hospital You were in the hospital for treatment of heart failure. Heart failure occurs when the heart muscles are weakened, have difficulty relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through the body. As with any pump, if the flow from it is insufficient, fluids circulate poorly and accumulate where they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid accumulates in the lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your medical team carefully monitored the amount of fluid you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) catheter. They also monitored and measured how much urine you produced. You may have been prescribed medications to help the body get rid of excess fluid. You may have had tests to check how well your heart is working. What to expect at home Your strength will gradually return. At first at home, you may need help caring for yourself. You may feel sad or depressed. All of this is normal. Self-monitoring at home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up ‚Äî before eating, but after using the toilet. Make sure you are dressed approximately the same each time you weigh yourself. Record your weight daily in a chart to track it. Throughout the day, ask yourself questions: Is my energy level normal? Am I getting more short of breath when doing everyday activities? Have my clothes or shoes become tighter? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Am I getting short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you have new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something unusual or try new food? Did I take all my medications cor...

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Heart Failure ‚Äî Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart can no longer effectively pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body. When symptoms become severe, a stay in the hospital may be required. This article tells you what you need to do to take care of yourself after discharge from the hospital. During the stay in the hospital You were in the hospital for the treatment of heart failure. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak, or it is difficult for them to relax, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluid through your body. As with any pump, if the outflow from the pump is insufficient, fluid moves poorly and gets stuck where it should not be. In your body, this means that fluid accumulates in the lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: * Your treating team carefully regulated the amount of fluid you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) drip. They also observed and measured how much urine you produced. * You may have received medicines helping your body get rid of excess fluid. * You may have had examinations conducted to check how well your heart is working. What to expect at home Your energy will return slowly. Perhaps you will need help caring for yourself when you first return home. You may feel sadness or depressed. All this is normal. Self-monitoring at home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up ‚Äî before eating, but after visiting the toilet. Make sure that every time you weigh yourself, you are wearing similar clothes. Write down your weight every day in a table so that you can track it. During the day, ask yourself: * Is my energy level normal? * Does severe shortness of breath appear when I do my daily activities? * Have clothes or shoes become tight? * Are my ankles or legs swelling? * Have I started coughing more often? Item: EVAL_0037 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Back-Translation (to English):

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that reduces the likelihood of blood clots (blood clumps) forming. It is very important to take warfarin exactly as you were told. Changing your warfarin regimen, taking other medicines together with it or at a different time, as well as consuming certain foods can change the effect of warfarin in your body. If this happens, your risk of developing a clot or having bleeding may increase. What to expect at home Warfarin is a medicine that reduces the likelihood of blood clots. This may be important if: You have already had clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your treating doctor is concerned that a clot may form in your body. People with a new heart valve, an enlarged heart, an abnormal heart rhythm, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you take warfarin, you may have a higher risk of bleeding, even with activities that are normal for you. Changing your warfarin regimen, taking other medicines, and consuming certain foods can change the effect of warfarin in your body. If this happens, your risk of developing a clot or having bleeding may increase. Taking warfarin It is very important to take warfarin exactly as you were told. Take only the dose prescribed by your doctor. If you missed a dose, contact your doctor for advice. If your tablets look different from the last time by prescription, contact your doctor or pharmacist immediately. Tablets have different colors depending on the dose. The dose is also indicated on the tablet. Your doctor will regularly check your blood. This test is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure that you are taking the right amount of warfarin for your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change the effect of warfarin in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Consult your doctor before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold remedies, antibiotics, or other medicines. Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Back-Translation (to English):

Taking Warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that reduces the blood's ability to form clots. It is important to take warfarin strictly according to your doctor's instructions. Changing your warfarin regimen, taking other medicines at the same time or at different times, as well as consuming certain foods can change how warfarin works in your body. In this case, the risk of forming a clot or bleeding may increase. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that reduces the blood's ability to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your healthcare provider is concerned that a clot may form in your body. Taking warfarin may be required for people with an artificial heart valve, an enlarged heart, heart rhythm disorders, or other heart problems. While taking warfarin, the risk of bleeding is increased, even when performing routine activities. Changing your warfarin regimen, taking other medicines, and consuming certain foods ‚Äî all of this can change how warfarin works in your body. In this case, the risk of forming a clot or bleeding may increase. Taking Warfarin It is important to take warfarin strictly according to your doctor's instructions. Take only the dose your doctor prescribed. If you missed a dose, contact your doctor for advice. If your tablets look different than in the previous prescription, immediately contact your doctor or pharmacist. Tablets differ in color depending on the dosage. The dose is also indicated on the tablet. Your doctor will regularly check your blood. This is called an INR (international normalized ratio) test or sometimes a PT (prothrombin time) test. This test helps ensure that you are taking the correct dose of warfarin for your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT consume alcohol while taking warfarin. Consult your doctor before taking any over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, dietary supplements, cold remedies, antibiotics...

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Taking Warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that reduces the likelihood of the formation of thrombi (blood clots). It is important to take warfarin exactly in accordance with the doctor's instructions. Changing the way of taking warfarin, taking other medicines simultaneously with it or at another time, as well as the consumption of certain food products can change the action of warfarin in your body. If this happens, your risk of forming clots or the occurrence of bleeding may increase. What to expect at home Warfarin is a medicine that reduces the likelihood of forming clots. This may be important if: * You have already had clots in the legs, arms, heart, or brain. * Your attending doctor is concerned that a clot may form in your body. People with an artificial heart valve, enlarged heart, heart rhythm disturbance, or other heart problems may require taking warfarin. During the intake of warfarin, your likelihood of bleeding may increase, even when performing habitual actions. Changing the way of taking warfarin, taking other medicines, and consuming certain food products ‚Äî all this can change the action of warfarin in your body. If this happens, your risk of forming clots or the occurrence of bleeding may increase. Taking warfarin It is important to take warfarin exactly in accordance with instructions. * Take only that dose which your doctor prescribed. If you missed taking a dose, turn to the doctor for advice. * If your tablets look different than in the past prescription, immediately contact the doctor or pharmacist. Tablets have a different color depending on the dosage. The dosage is also indicated on the tablet itself. * Your doctor will check your blood during regular visits. This is called an INR analysis (international normalized ratio) or sometimes a PT test (prothrombin time). Original English:

Heart attack ‚Äì discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Do not change your exercise plan before talking with y...

Back-Translation (to English):

Myocardial infarction ‚Äì discharge Myocardial infarction occurs when the blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked for long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article explains what you need to do to take care of yourself after discharge from the hospital. When you are in the hospital You were in the hospital because you had a myocardial infarction. Myocardial infarction occurs when the blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked for long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to expect at home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and think that you need to be very careful in everything you do. All these feelings are normal. In most people they pass within 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you are discharged from the hospital and return home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, and abdominal area. You may have heartburn or nausea. You may feel tired, short of breath, sweaty, dizzy, or weak. Angina may occur during physical exertion, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting heavy objects, sexual intercourse, as well as when being out in the cold. It may also occur at rest or wake you from sleep. Know how to treat chest pain when it occurs. Discuss with your health care provider what needs to be done. During the first 4‚Äì6 weeks after myocardial infarction you need to take care of yourself. Avoid lifting heavy objects. Ask for help with housework if possible. Rest for 30‚Äì60 minutes during the day for the first 4‚Äì6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get enough sleep. Before starting to exercise, your doctor may perform a stress test and recommend an exercise plan. This may be done before discharge from the hospital or soon after it. Do ...

Original English:

Heart attack ‚Äì discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Do not change your exercise plan before talking with y...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart attack - hospital discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of the heart is blocked long enough, and part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article explains what you need to do to care for yourself after discharge from the hospital. When you were in the hospital You were in the hospital because of a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of the heart is blocked long enough, and part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to expect at home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and feel that you need to be very careful in everything you do. All these feelings are normal. For most people, they go away within 2-3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you return home from the hospital. Physical activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have an upset stomach or nausea. You may feel tired, short of breath, sweaty, dizzy, or weak. Angina may occur during physical activity such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting heavy objects, sexual activity, or when you are in the cold. It can also occur at rest or wake you during sleep. Know how to deal with chest pain when it occurs. Discuss with your healthcare provider what you should do. Follow a gentle regimen for the first 4-6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid lifting heavy objects. If possible, ask for help with household chores. For the first 4-6 weeks, rest for 30-60 minutes in the afternoon. Try to go to bed early and get a good night's sleep. Before starting exercise, your doctor may perform a stress test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before discharge from the hospital or soon after. Do not change your exercise plan without consulting your doctor. Your doctor may refer you to...

Original English:

Heart attack ‚Äì discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Item: EVAL_0085 | Document: CARD_004 | Model: Model C

Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? Back-Translation (to English):

High blood pressure ‚Äì what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into the arteries, the pressure of the blood on the walls of the arteries is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is recorded with two numbers: systolic and diastolic pressure. Your systolic pressure is the highest blood pressure during one cycle of a heartbeat. Your diastolic pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure becomes too high, it creates additional strain on the heart and blood vessels. If your pressure constantly remains high, your risk of myocardial infarction and other vascular (blood vessel diseases), stroke, kidney disease and other health problems increases. Below are questions that you can ask your attending physician to better control your blood pressure. Questions How can I change my lifestyle to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart‚Äëhealthy diet? Is it possible to sometimes eat food that is not heart‚Äëhealthy? How can I eat healthier when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit the amount of salt? Are there other seasonings that I can use so that the food is tasty? May I consume alcohol? How much is allowed? What can I do to quit smoking? May I be around other people who smoke? Do I need to measure my blood pressure at home? What equipment do I need to buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to measure my blood pressure? Do I need to write down the readings and bring them to the next appointment? If I cannot measure my blood pressure myself, where else can I have it measured? What should my blood pressure be? Do I need to rest before measuring my pressure? When should I contact a doctor? What is my cholesterol level? Do I need to take medicine to lower it? May I be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn) or avanafil (Stendra) for problems with erection? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? Do they have any side effects? What...

Back-Translation (to English):

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When the heart pumps blood into the arteries, the pressure of blood on the walls of the arteries is called blood pressure. Blood pressure is recorded as two numbers: systolic over diastolic. Systolic blood pressure is the maximum blood pressure during the cardiac contraction cycle. Diastolic blood pressure is the minimum pressure. When blood pressure becomes too high, it creates additional strain on the heart and blood vessels. If blood pressure remains constantly high, you have an increased risk of heart attacks and other vascular diseases, strokes, kidney diseases, and other health problems. Below are questions you can ask your healthcare provider to better control your blood pressure. Questions How can I change my lifestyle to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Can I sometimes eat something that is not heart-healthy? How can I eat healthy food when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit my salt intake? Are there other spices I can use to make food tasty? Can I drink alcohol? How much is acceptable? What can I do to quit smoking? Can I be around people who smoke? Do I need to measure my blood pressure at home? What equipment should I get? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to measure my blood pressure? Do I need to record the readings and bring them to my next appointment? If I cannot measure my blood pressure myself, where else can I have it measured? What should my blood pressure be? Do I need to rest before measuring my blood pressure? When should I contact my doctor? What is my cholesterol level? Do I need to take medication to lower it? Can I have sexual activity? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medications am I taking to treat high blood pressure? Do they have side effects? What should I do if I miss a dose? Can I ever stop taking any of these medications on my own? Wh...

Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? Do they have any side effects? What...

Back-Translation (to English):

High Blood Pressure: What to Ask Your Doctor When your heart pumps blood into the arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called blood pressure. Your pressure is recorded as two numbers: systolic and diastolic pressure. Systolic pressure is the highest pressure during the cardiac cycle (heartbeat). Diastolic pressure is the lowest pressure. When blood pressure becomes too high, it creates additional load on the heart and blood vessels. If pressure remains high constantly, your risk of heart attacks, other vascular diseases (diseases of blood vessels), strokes, kidney diseases, and other health problems increases. Below are questions that you can ask your attending doctor to help you control your blood pressure. Questions How should I change my lifestyle to lower blood pressure? * What is a heart-healthy diet? Can I sometimes eat something not very heart-healthy? How should I eat correctly when I go to a restaurant? * Do I need to limit salt consumption? Are there other spices that I can use so that food is tasty? * Can I consume alcohol? What amount is acceptable? * What can I do to quit smoking? Is it safe to be near people who smoke? Do I need to measure blood pressure at home? * What device should I buy? Where can I learn to use it? * How often do I need to measure pressure? Do I need to record the results and bring them to the next appointment? * If I cannot measure pressure on my own, where else can I do this? * What should my pressure readings be? Do I need to rest before measuring pressure? * In which cases should I contact the doctor? What is my cholesterol level? Do I need to take medicines to lower it? Can I be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? * Do they have side effects? What to do if I miss taking a medicine? * Is it ever safe to stop ...

Original English:

Type 2 diabetes - what to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in your blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage due to diabetes, and make your life better. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your diabetes. Questions Ask your provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulses in your feet. Also ask these questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I do when I check them? What problems should I call my provider about? Who should trim my toenails? Is it OK if I trim them? How should I take care of my feet every day? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? Should I see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your provider about getting exercise, including: Before I start, do I need to have my heart checked? My eyes? My feet? What type of exercise program should I do? What type of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I bring with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should I next have an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact my eye doctor about? Ask your provider about meeting with a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: What foods increase my blood sugar the most? What foods can help me with my weight loss goals? Ask your provider about your diabetes medicines: When should I take them? What should I do if I miss a dose? Are there any side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar level at home? Should I do it at different times of the day? What is too low? What is too high? What should I do if my blood sugar is too low or too high? Should I get a medical alert bracelet or necklace? Back-Translation (to English):

Type 2 diabetes ‚Äì what questions to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, after it has been diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in the blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage from diabetes, and improve the quality of your life. Below are questions that you can ask your healthcare provider to help yourself in caring for your diabetes. Questions Ask your doctor or other healthcare provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulse in your feet. Also ask the following questions: How often do I need to examine my feet? What should I do when I examine them? For what foot problems do I need to call the doctor? Who should trim my toenails? Can I trim them myself? How do I take care of my feet every day? What shoes and what socks should I wear? Do I need to see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your doctor about physical exercise, including: Do I need to have my heart checked before starting activities? My eyes? My feet? What exercise program should I follow? What types of activity should I avoid? When do I need to check my blood sugar level when I exercise? What should I take with me when I exercise? Do I need to eat before or during exercise? Do I need to change my medicine dose when I exercise? When do I next need to have an eye exam by an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist)? For what eye problems do I need to contact the eye doctor? Ask your doctor about a consultation with a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: Which foods raise my blood sugar level the most? Which foods can help me achieve my weight loss goals? Ask your doctor about your diabetes medicines: When should I take them? What should I do if I missed a dose? Are there any side effects? How often do I need to check my blood sugar level at home? Do I need to do this at different times of the day? What level is too low? W...

Original English:

Type 2 diabetes - what to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in your blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage due to diabetes, and make your life better. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your diabetes. Questions Ask your provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulses in your feet. Also ask these questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I do when I check them? What problems should I call my provider about? Who should trim my toenails? Is it OK if I trim them? How should I take care of my feet every day? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? Should I see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your provider about getting exercise, including: Before I start, do I need to have my heart checked? My eyes? My feet? What type of exercise program should I do? What type of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I bring with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should I next have an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact my eye doctor about? Ask your provider about meeting with a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: What foods increase my blood sugar the most? What foods can help me with my weight loss goals? Ask your provider about your diabetes medicines: When should I take them? What should I do if I miss a dose? Are there any side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar level at home? Should I do it at different times of the day? What is too low? What is too high? What should I do if my blood sugar is too low or too high? Should I get a medical alert bracelet or necklace? Type 2 diabetes - what to ask the doctor Type 2 diabetes, after diagnosis, is a lifelong disease that causes elevated sugar (glucose) levels in the blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control symptoms, prevent diabetes complications, and improve your quality of life. Below are questions you can ask your healthcare provider to better control your diabetes. Questions Ask the doctor to check the nerves, skin, and pulse on your feet. Also ask the following questions: How often should I examine my feet? What should I do during the examination? What problems should I report to the doctor? Who should trim my toenails? Can I do it myself? How should I care for my feet every day? What shoes and socks should I wear? Do I need to see a podiatrist (foot disease specialist)? Ask the doctor about physical exercises, including: Do I need to check my heart before starting exercises? Eyes? Feet? What exercises are right for me? What types of activity should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar during exercises? What should I bring with me to workout? Do I need to eat before or during exercises? Do I need to adjust medication when exercising? When do I next need to have my vision checked by an ophthalmologist (eye doctor)? For what eye problems should I see an ophthalmologist? Ask the doctor about consultation with a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: What foods raise blood sugar the most? What foods will help me achieve my weight loss goals? Ask the doctor about your diabetes medications: When should I take them? What should I do if I missed a dose? Are there side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar at home? Do I need to do it at different times of day? What level is considered too low? What is too high? What should I do if blood sugar is too low or too high? Original English:

Type 2 diabetes - what to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in your blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage due to diabetes, and make your life better. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your diabetes. Questions Ask your provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulses in your feet. Also ask these questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I do when I check them? What problems should I call my provider about? Who should trim my toenails? Is it OK if I trim them? How should I take care of my feet every day? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? Should I see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your provider about getting exercise, including: Before I start, do I need to have my heart checked? My eyes? My feet? What type of exercise program should I do? What type of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I bring with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should I next have an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact my eye doctor about? Ask your provider about meeting with a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: What foods increase my blood sugar the most? What foods can help me with my weight loss goals? Ask your provider about your diabetes medicines: When should I take them? What should I do if I miss a dose? Are there any side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar level at home? Should I do it at different times of the day? What is too low? What is too high? What should I do if my blood sugar is too low or too high? Should I get a medical alert bracelet or necklace? Item: EVAL_0133 | Document: DIAB_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Low blood sugar ‚Äì self-help Low blood sugar is a condition in which the level of sugar (glucose) in the blood is below normal. Low blood sugar can occur in people with diabetes who take insulin or some other medicines to control diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and prevent them. What is low blood sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is considered low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) requires immediate action. You are at risk of low blood sugar if you have diabetes and you take any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of low blood sugar if you have already had episodes of low blood sugar. Recognizing low blood sugar Know how to understand that your blood sugar level is dropping. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shakiness Sweating Headache Feeling hungry Feeling worried, nervous, or anxious Irritability Difficulty thinking clearly Double vision or blurry vision Rapid or strong heartbeat Sometimes blood sugar can be too low even if you have no symptoms. If it becomes too low, you may: Lose consciousness Have seizures Fall into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time lose the ability to feel low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider whether wearing a continuous glucose monitoring system and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar becomes too low, in order to prevent symptoms. Check your blood sugar more often Discuss with your health care provider when you need to check your blood sugar each day. People with low blood sugar need to check it more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar: Taking insulin or diabetes medicine at th...

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Low blood sugar ‚Äî self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when the blood sugar (glucose) level drops below normal. Low blood sugar can occur in people with diabetes who take insulin or certain other medicines to control diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is low blood sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) requires immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and take any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk for low blood sugar if you have had episodes of low blood sugar before. Recognizing low blood sugar Learn to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Irritability Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time no longer feel low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemia unawareness. Ask your health care provider if using a continuous glucose monitoring system with a sensor can help you detect falling blood sugar to prevent symptoms. Check your blood sugar often Talk to your doctor about when you should check your blood sugar each day. People with low blood sugar need to check it more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insulin or diabetes medicine Giving insulin to correct high blood sugar without eating food Not e...

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Low Blood Sugar ‚Äî Self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when the level of sugar (glucose) in your blood is below normal. Low sugar can occur in people with diabetes who take insulin or certain other medicines to control diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize symptoms of low sugar levels and how to prevent them. What is low blood sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) requires immediate action. You are in the risk group for low sugar level if you have diabetes and you take any of the following medicines for diabetes: * Insulin * Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin) or nateglinide (Starlix) The risk also increases if your blood sugar level has dropped before. How to recognize low blood sugar Know how to determine that blood sugar level is falling. Symptoms include: * Weakness or feeling tired * Shaking * Sweating * Headache * Feeling of hunger * Restlessness, nervousness or anxiety * Irritability * Difficulty thinking clearly * Double vision or blurry vision * Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes the sugar level can be too low even in the absence of symptoms. If it drops too low, you may: * Lose consciousness * Experience seizures * Fall into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop feeling the drop in sugar. This is called hypoglycemia unawareness. Ask your treating doctor if wearing a continuous glucose monitor (sensor) will help you detect a drop in sugar level and prevent symptoms. Check blood sugar level more often Discuss with the doctor when you should check sugar level during the day. People with low sugar level need to check it more often. The most frequent causes of low blood sugar: * Taking insulin or diabetes medicines at the wrong time * Taking too large a...

Item: EVAL_0157 | Document: RESP_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Back-Translation (to English):

Asthma ‚Äì fast-acting medications Fast-acting medications for asthma act quickly to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, experiencing shortness of breath, or having an asthma attack. They are also called ‚Äúemergency medications‚Äù or ‚Äúrescue medications.‚Äù Many of these medications are called ‚Äúbronchodilators‚Äù because they widen (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your healthcare provider can create a plan for taking fast-acting medications that are right for you. This plan will include when you should take them and in what dose. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out of medications. Take with you a sufficient amount of the medication when you travel. Short-acting beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common fast-acting medications for treating asthma attacks and are considered bronchodilators. They can be used right before physical exertion to prevent asthma symptoms caused by exertion. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, which allows you to breathe better during an attack. Tell your doctor if you are using fast-acting medications two times a week or more often to control asthma symptoms. Your asthma may be insufficiently controlled, and the doctor may need to change the dose of your medications for daily control. Some fast-acting medications for asthma include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists can cause the following side effects: Anxiety. Rapid and irregular heartbeat. Call your doctor immediately if you develop this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral steroids Your doctor may prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that does not go away. These are medications that you take by mouth in the form of tablets, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not fast-a...

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Back-Translation (to English):

Asthma - quick-relief medications Quick-relief medications for asthma act quickly, controlling asthma symptoms. You take them for coughing, wheezing, difficulty breathing, or an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medications. Many of these medications are called "bronchodilators" because they dilate and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your healthcare provider can create a plan for taking quick-relief medications that are right for you. This plan will include information about when to take them and how much. Plan ahead. Make sure you don't run out of medications. Bring enough medications with you when you travel. Short-acting beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medications for treating asthma attacks and are bronchodilators. They can be used just before physical activity to prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the airway muscles, which allows you to breathe better during an attack. Tell your doctor if you use quick-relief medications two times a week or more often to control asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your doctor may need to change the dose of your daily controller medications. Some quick-relief asthma medications include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause the following side effects: Anxiety. Rapid and irregular heartbeat. Contact your doctor immediately if this side effect occurs. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (shaking of the hand or other body part). Oral steroids Your doctor may prescribe oral steroids for an asthma attack that does not go away. These are medications that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medications, but are often prescribed for 7-14 days when symptoms flare up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Item: EVAL_0159 | Document: RESP_001 | Model: Model B

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Asthma ‚Äî Quick-Relief Medications Quick-relief medications for asthma start working quickly, helping to cope with symptoms.
