They can be used immediately before physical exertion to prevent asthma symptoms caused by physical activity. They relax the muscles of the airways, which allows you to breathe easier during an attack. Inform your doctor if you use quick-relief medications two times a week or more often to control asthma symptoms. Possibly, your asthma is not under control, and the doctor will need to change the dosage of your medications for daily control of the disease. Item: EVAL_0181 | Document: RESP_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk around very much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save some of my energy around the house...

Back-Translation (to English):

COPD ‚Äì what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. Because of this, it may be hard for you to get enough oxygen and remove carbon dioxide from your lungs. Although COPD cannot be completely cured, you can do a lot to control your symptoms and improve your quality of life. Below are questions you can ask your healthcare provider to take better care of your lungs. Questions What can make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent a lung infection from developing? How can I get help to quit smoking? Can fumes, dust, or pets make my COPD worse? What signs indicate that my breathing is getting worse and I need to contact a doctor? What should I do when I feel short of breath? Am I taking my COPD medicines correctly? Which medicines should I take every day (the so‚Äëcalled maintenance medicines, or controller medicines)? What should I do if I missed a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (the so‚Äëcalled fast‚Äëacting medicines or rescue medicines, quick‚Äërelief or rescue medicines)? Can these medicines be used every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? With which side effects do I need to contact a doctor? Am I using my inhaler correctly? Do I need to use a spacer? How will I know that my inhaler is almost empty? When should I use a nebulizer, and when an inhaler? What changes do I need to make at home? What changes do I need to make at work? Which shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all the time? Are there any changes in nutrition that will help with COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning a trip? Will I need oxygen on the plane? And in the airport? Which medicines should I take with me? Whom should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What exercises can I do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk much? Should I think about pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I conserve my energy at home?...

Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk around very much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save some of my energy around the house...

Back-Translation (to English):

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. Because of this, it may be difficult for you to get enough oxygen and remove carbon dioxide from your lungs. Although COPD cannot be completely cured, you can do a lot to control your symptoms and improve your quality of life. Below are questions you can ask your healthcare provider to better take care of your lungs. Questions What can make my COPD worse? How do I avoid factors that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Can fumes, dust, or pets make my COPD worse? What signs indicate that my breathing is getting worse and I should see a doctor? What should I do when I feel like I can't get enough air? Am I taking my COPD medications correctly? What medications should I take every day (called maintenance medications)? What if I miss a day or a dose? What medications should I take for shortness of breath (called quick-acting medications or rescue medications)? Can these medications be used every day? What are the side effects of my medications? For what side effects should I see a doctor? Am I using my inhaler correctly? Do I need to use a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are running out? When should I use a nebulizer and when should I use an inhaler? What changes should I make at home? What changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If so, do I need it all the time? Are there dietary changes that will help with my COPD? What do I need to do when I'm planning a trip? Will I need oxygen on the plane? And at the airport? What medications should I take with me? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What exercises can I do to keep my muscles in shape, even if I can't walk much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save energy when doing housework? Am I at increased risk for COVID-19 or other diseases? How do I protect myself?

Item: EVAL_0183 | Document: RESP_002 | Model: Model B

Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? COPD ‚Äî what to ask the doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. Because of this, it may be difficult for you to get enough oxygen and remove carbon dioxide from the lungs. Although COPD is incurable, you can do a lot to control symptoms and improve quality of life. Below are questions that you can ask your doctor to help you care for your lungs. What should I do if I miss a day or a dose of medicine? * What medicines should I take for shortness of breath (so-called drugs for rapid relief of symptoms or "emergency aid")? Is it possible to use these medicines every day? * What are the side effects of my medicines? For which side effects should I see a doctor? * Am I using the inhaler correctly? Do I need to use a spacer? How will I know that the medicine in the inhaler is running out? * When should I use a nebulizer, and when ‚Äî an inhaler? What changes should I make to the home environment? What changes do I need to make at work? What inoculations or vaccination are necessary for me? Do I need oxygen? If yes, then do I need it constantly? Are there changes in nutrition that will help with COPD? What do I need to do if I am planning a trip? * Will I need oxygen on the plane? And at the airport? * What medicines should I take with me? * Who should I contact if the course of COPD worsens? * What exercises can I do to maintain muscle strength, even if I cannot walk much? Item: EVAL_0205 | Document: MED_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Back-Translation (to English):

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme inhibitors (ACE inhibitors) are medicines. They treat diseases of the heart, blood vessels, and kidneys. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart diseases and high blood pressure (hypertension). These medicines lower your blood pressure and reduce the workload on the heart. This helps prevent the worsening of some types of heart disease. Most people with heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They can help reduce the risk of stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney diseases. This can help slow the worsening of kidney function or even improve their function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your healthcare provider whether you need to take these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brand names of ACE inhibitors. Most of them work equally well. Side effects may differ between different medicines. Taking ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors come in the form of tablets that you take by mouth. Take all your medicines exactly as your doctor told you. Visit your doctor regularly. Your doctor will measure your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. The doctor may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take the medicines at the same time every day. Do not stop taking the medicines without talking to your doctor first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicines. Make sure you have enough tablets with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your doctor. Tell your doctor about all the medicines you are taking, including those you bought without a prescription, diuretics (‚Äúwater tablets‚Äù), potassium tablets, or herbal and dietary supplements. Do not take ACE inhibitors if you are planning a pregnancy, are pregnant, or are breastfe...

Item: EVAL_0206 | Document: MED_001 | Model: Model A

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

ACE Inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medications. They are used to treat diseases of the heart, blood vessels, and kidneys. How ACE Inhibitors Help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medications reduce the workload on the heart by lowering blood pressure. This prevents the worsening of certain types of heart diseases. Most people with heart failure take one of these medications or similar drugs. These medications are used to treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They can help reduce the risk of stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney disease. This can help prevent worsening of kidney function or even improve their function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your healthcare provider whether you should take these medications. Types of ACE Inhibitors There are many different names and brand names of ACE inhibitors. Most of them work equally effectively. Side effects may vary between different medications. How to Take ACE Inhibitors ACE inhibitors are tablets that are taken by mouth. Take all medications as prescribed by your doctor. Visit your doctor regularly for monitoring. The doctor will check your blood pressure and perform blood tests to make sure the medications are working properly. The doctor may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take medications at the same time every day. Do not stop taking medications without talking to your doctor first. Plan ahead so you do not run out of medications. Make sure you have enough medications with you during trips. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, consult with your doctor. Tell your doctor what other medications you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium preparations, or herbal and dietary supplements. Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Back-Translation (to English):

ACE Inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicinal drugs. They treat problems with the heart, blood vessels, and kidneys. **How ACE inhibitors help** ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart diseases and high blood pressure. These medicines reduce the load on the heart by lowering blood pressure. This prevents the worsening of certain types of heart diseases. Most people with heart failure take one of these drugs or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They can help reduce the risk of stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help prevent the worsening of the condition of the kidneys or even improve their function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your attending doctor if you should take these medicines. **Types of ACE inhibitors** There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most of them work equally well. Side effects of different drugs may differ. **Taking ACE inhibitors** ACE inhibitors are pills that are taken orally. Take all medicines as the doctor told you. Visit the doctor regularly. The doctor will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure that the medicines are working correctly. From time to time, the doctor may change the dosage. In addition: * Try to take the medicines at the same time every day. * Do not stop taking the medicines without consulting the doctor first. * Plan ahead so that the medicines do not run out. Make sure that you have enough medicines when you go on a trip. * Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to the doctor. * Tell the doctor what other medicines you are taking, including everything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium preparations, as well as herbal or dietary supplements. * Do not take ACE inhibitors if you plan to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Item: EVAL_0229 | Document: EMER_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Family and caregivers may need to help with: Exercises to keep your elbows, shoulders, and other joints loose Watching for joint tightening (contractures) Making sure splints are used in the correct way Making sure arms and legs are in a good position when sitting or lying If you or your loved one is using a wheelchair, fol...

Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke ‚Äì discharge from the hospital You were in the hospital after a stroke. A stroke happens when the blood flow to some part of the brain stops. At home, follow the self‚Äëcare instructions given to you by your healthcare provider. Use the information below as a reminder. When you are in the hospital First you were given treatment to prevent further brain damage and to help your heart, lungs, and other important organs recover. After your condition stabilized, you were examined and treatment was started to help you recover from the stroke and prevent another stroke. You may have been in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to expect at home Because of possible brain damage from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing simple tasks Memory Movement of one side of the body Muscle spasms Maintaining attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Speech or understanding others Thinking Vision on one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities that you did independently before the stroke. You may have a course of physical therapy or occupational therapy (ergotherapy) to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is quite common as you learn to live with the changes. It can develop soon after the stroke or within 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive a car without your doctor‚Äôs permission. Mobility Moving around and doing usual activities after a stroke can be difficult. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your doctor, therapist, or nurse to suggest what changes can be made at home to make it easier for you to do everyday tasks. Find out what you can do to prevent falls and make the bathroom safe to use. Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Family and caregivers may need to help with: Exercises to keep your elbows, shoulders, and other joints loose Watching for joint tightening (contractures) Making sure splints are used in the correct way Making sure arms and legs are in a good position when sitting or lying If you or your loved one is using a wheelchair, fol...

Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke - hospital discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood supply to part of the brain stops. At home, follow your healthcare provider's instructions for self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When you were in the hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further brain damage and to restore the function of the heart, lungs, and other vital organs. After your condition stabilized, doctors performed examinations and began treatment aimed at recovery after stroke and preventing another stroke. You may have been in a specialized stroke rehabilitation unit. What to expect at home Due to possible brain damage as a result of stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Performing simple tasks Memory Movement of one side of the body Muscle spasms Concentration Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Speech or understanding other people Thinking Vision to one side (hemianopia) You may need help with daily activities that you used to do by yourself before the stroke. You may be prescribed physical therapy or occupational therapy to restore the ability to perform these functions. Depression after stroke is quite common when you are learning to live with changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or within 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive without permission from your doctor. Getting around Getting around and performing usual activities may be difficult after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your doctor, rehabilitation specialist, or nurse about what changes to make in your home to make performing daily activities easier. Learn what you can do to prevent falls and how to ensure safety in the bathroom. Family members and caregivers may need to help with: Exercises to maintain mobility of elbows, shoulders, and other joints Watching for joint stiffness (contractures) Monitoring proper use of splints Monitoring proper positioning of arms and legs when sit...

Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke ‚Äî Discharge You were in the hospital after suffering a stroke. A stroke occurs when blood flow to a part of the brain stops. At home, follow the instructions of your attending physician on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. **Hospital Stay** First, you received treatment to prevent further brain damage and help the heart, lungs, and other important organs recover. After your condition stabilized, doctors conducted an examination and started treatment to help you recover after the stroke and prevent a recurrent stroke. You may have been in a special unit for stroke recovery. **What to expect at home** Due to possible brain damage resulting from the stroke, you may have problems with: * Changes in behavior * Performing simple tasks * Memory * Movement of one side of the body * Muscle spasms * Concentration of attention * Sensation or feeling in one part of the body * Swallowing * Speech or understanding other people * Thinking * Vision on one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities that you did on your own before the stroke. You may be prescribed physical therapy or occupational therapy to restore the ability to perform these actions. Depression after a stroke occurs quite often while you are learning to live with the changes. It can develop soon after the stroke or within 2 years after it. Do not get behind the wheel of a car without the doctor's permission. **Moving around** Moving around and performing ordinary tasks after a stroke can be difficult. Make sure your home is safe. Ask the doctor, therapist, or nurse about what changes need to be made to the home environment to make performing daily activities easier. Find out what can be done to prevent falls and ensure safety in the bathroom. Family and caregivers may need to help with: * Exercises to maintain mobility of elbows, shoulders, and other joints * Monitoring for joint stiffness (contractures) * Controlling the correct use...

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Care for a surgical wound ‚Äì open wound An incision is a cut in the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on what kind of surgery was performed. Sometimes the incision opens up. This can happen along the entire length of the incision or only on part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with stitches. What to expect at home If your surgeon did not close the wound again with stitches, you need to care for it at home, since healing may take time. The wound will heal from the bottom up. The dressing helps absorb discharge and prevents the skin from closing before the wound underneath is filled in. Proper handwashing It is important to clean your hands before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol-based antiseptic. Or you can wash your hands by following these steps: Remove all jewelry from your hands. Wet your hands, holding them with the fingers pointing down under warm running water. Apply soap and wash your hands for 15‚Äì30 seconds (sing the ‚ÄúHappy Birthday‚Äù song or the ‚ÄúAlphabet Song‚Äù once all the way through). Clean under the nails as well. Rinse the soap off thoroughly. Dry your hands with a clean towel. Removing the old dressing Your healthcare provider will tell you how often you need to change the dressing. To prepare for changing the dressing: Wash your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure all necessary supplies are within reach. Prepare a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully separate the tape (adhesive bandage) from the skin. Use a clean (non-sterile) medical glove to pick up the old dressing and remove it. If the dressing is stuck to the wound, wet it and try to remove it again, unless your doctor has told you to remove it dry. Place the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Wash your hands again after you have removed the old dressing. Wound care You ...

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Surgical wound care ‚Äî open wound An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery performed. Sometimes an incision comes apart. This may happen along the entire length of the incision or only in part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with stitches. What to expect at home If your surgeon does not close the wound again with stitches, you will need to care for it at home because it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom up. A dressing helps absorb drainage and prevents the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper hand washing It is important to wash your hands before changing the dressing. You may use an alcohol-based hand sanitizer. Or you may wash your hands by following these steps: Remove all jewelry from your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Apply soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing the "Happy Birthday" or "Alphabet" song once). Also clean under your fingernails. Rinse your hands well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the old dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for a dressing change: Wash your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies you need within reach. Prepare a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Gently peel the tape away from the skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and remove it. If the dressing sticks to the wound, moisten it and try again, unless your provider told you to remove it dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Wash your hands again after removing the old dressing. Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Back-Translation (to English):

Care for an Open Surgical Wound An incision is a breach of the integrity of the skin made during an operation. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of operation you underwent. Sometimes an incision comes apart. This can happen along the entire length of the incision or only in a part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to sew it up again with stitches. **What to expect at home** If the surgeon does not suture the wound again, you will need to care for it at home, as it may take time for it to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom up. The dressing helps absorb discharge and keeps the skin from closing before the wound fills in from the inside. **Proper hand washing** It is important to wash your hands before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol-based hand product. Or you can wash your hands by following these steps: 1. Remove all jewelry from your hands. 2. Wet your hands with warm running water, holding them with fingers pointing down. 3. Lather your hands and rub them for 15‚Äì30 seconds (sing the "Happy Birthday to You" song to yourself or recite the alphabet once). Clean under the fingernails as well. 4. Rinse the soap off well. 5. Dry your hands with a clean towel. **Removing the old dressing** Your treating doctor will tell you how often you need to change the dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: * Wash your hands before touching the dressing. * Make sure you have all necessary materials close at hand. * Prepare a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: * Carefully peel the tape off the skin. * Use a clean (non-sterile) medical glove to take the old dressing and remove it. * If the dressing is stuck to the wound, wet it and try again, unless the doctor told you to remove it dry. * Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. * Wash your hands again after you have removed the old dressing. **Wound care** You can use ...

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

This memo explains what needs to be done to care for the new joint after discharge from the hospital. You need to be careful not to dislocate the artificial hip joint, especially in the first few months after the operation. You will need to learn exercises to strengthen the new joint and follow special precautions. Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Replacement - Discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint, called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip after you leave the hospital. During Your Hospital Stay You had hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches with little help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Ask your surgeon when you can stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress with minimal help and get in and out of a bed or chair on your own. You should also be able to use the toilet with almost no help. You will need to be careful not to dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that strengthen your new joint and follow special precautions. You will need someone to be with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehabilitation center. You will need help with cooking, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily tasks. Over time, you will be able to return to your previous level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports, for example, football and soccer. But you will be able to do low-impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, tennis, and golf. Setting Up Your Home Your bed should be low enough that your feet touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough that your hips are above your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but the mattress should be firm. Remove anything from your home that you could trip over. Learn how to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cables from paths you walk from room to room. Remove unsecured rugs. Do not keep small pets at home...

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Hip replacement ‚Äî discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of the hip joint with an artificial joint, which is called a prosthesis. This article will tell you what needs to be done to care for the new joint after discharge from the hospital. **In the hospital** You had surgery for hip replacement to replace all or part of the joint with an artificial one. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. **What to expect at home** By the moment of returning home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without much help. Most people stop needing them in 2‚Äì4 weeks. Check with the surgeon when you can stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress with minimal help, as well as independently get into bed and get up from it or from a chair. You should also be able to use the toilet without outside help. You need to be careful not to allow dislocation of the artificial joint, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises to strengthen the new joint and observe special precautions. You will need someone to stay with you at home for 1‚Äì2 weeks after discharge from the hospital or rehabilitation center. You will require help with cooking food, bathing, moving around the house, and performing other daily chores. Over time, you will be able to return to your previous level of activity. You will have to avoid certain sports, such as alpine skiing or contact sports (for example, American football and soccer). But you will be able to engage in low-impact activities, such as walking, gardening, swimming, tennis, and golf. **Setting up the home** Your bed should be low enough so that your feet touch the floor when you sit on the edge. The bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but the mattress must be firm. Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Heart failure ‚Äî discharge from the hospital Heart failure is a condition in which the heart can no longer effectively pump oxygen-rich blood to the other parts of the body. When symptoms become severe, hospitalization may be required. This article explains what you need to do to take care of yourself when you are discharged from the hospital. When you are in the hospital You were in the hospital for treatment of heart failure. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weakened or it is difficult for them to relax, or both together. Your heart is a pump which moves fluids throughout the body. Like any pump, if the flow from the pump is insufficient, fluids move poorly and stagnate in places where they should not be. In your body this means that fluid accumulates in the lungs, abdominal cavity and legs. While you were in the hospital: ‚Ä¢ Your team of medical workers carefully regulated the amount of fluid which you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) drip. They also monitored how much urine you excreted and measured its amount. ‚Ä¢ You may have been given medications to help your body get rid of excess fluid. ‚Ä¢ You may have had tests done to check how your heart is working. What to expect at home Your energy will gradually return. You may need help in caring for yourself when you first return home. You may feel sad or depressed. All this is normal. Item: EVAL_0301 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Back-Translation (to English):

--- **Taking warfarin** Warfarin is a medication that reduces the tendency of your blood to form clots. It is important to take warfarin exactly as you were told. Changing the method of taking warfarin, taking other medications together with it or at a different time, as well as consumption of certain products can change the action of warfarin in your body. If this happens, your risk of clot formation or occurrence of bleedings may increase. **What to expect at home** Warfarin is a medication that reduces the tendency of your blood to form clots. This may be important if: - You have already had clots in the leg, arm, heart or brain. - Your doctor is concerned about the possibility of clot formation in your body. People with a new heart valve, enlarged heart, disturbance of heart rhythm or other problems with the heart may require taking warfarin. When you take warfarin, your probability of bleedings may increase, even from usual activities. Changing the method of taking warfarin, taking other medications and consumption of certain products can change the action of warfarin in your body. If this happens, your risk of clot formation or occurrence of bleedings may increase. **Taking warfarin** It is important to take warfarin exactly as you were told. - Take only that dose which the doctor prescribed to you. If you missed a dose, contact the doctor for advice. - If your tablets look different than last time, immediately contact the doctor or pharmacist. Tablets of different colors depending on the dose. The dose is also indicated on the tablet. - Your doctor will regularly check your blood. This is called an MNO analysis (international normalized ratio) or sometimes an analysis of prothrombin time. The analysis helps to ensure that you are taking the right amount of warfarin. **Alcohol and some medications can change the action of warfarin in your body.** - DO NOT consume alcohol while taking warfarin. - Talk with the doctor before taking any other non-presc...

Original English:

Heart attack ‚Äì discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Myocardial infarction ‚Äî discharge from the hospital Myocardial infarction occurs when blood flow in part of your heart is blocked for so long that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. In this article it is told what you need to do to take care of yourself after discharge from the hospital. When you are in the hospital You were in the hospital because you had a myocardial infarction. Myocardial infarction occurs when blood flow in part of your heart is blocked for so long that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to expect at home You may feel sadness. You may feel anxiety and a feeling that you must be very careful in your actions. All these feelings are normal. In most people they pass within 2‚Äì3 weeks. You may also feel fatigue when you are discharged from the hospital. Activity You must know the signs and symptoms of angina. - You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning or tightness in the chest. You may also notice these symptoms in the arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat or back. - Some people also feel discomfort in the back, shoulders and abdominal area. - You may have heartburn or nausea. - You may feel fatigue, shortness of breath, sweating, dizziness or weakness. Angina may occur during physical activity, for example, when climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting weights, sexual activity or being in the cold. It may also occur at rest or wake you during sleep. Know how to treat chest pain when it occurs. Talk with your treating doctor about what to do. Take care of yourself in the first 4‚Äì6 weeks after myocardial infarction. - Avoid lifting weights. If possible, ask for help with household chores. - Rest 30‚Äì60 minutes during the day in the first 4‚Äì6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get enough sleep. - Before starting sports activities your doctor may prescribe you a test with physical load and recommend an exercise plan. Item: EVAL_0317 | Document: CARD_004 | Model: Model D

Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? Do they have any side effects? What...

Back-Translation (to English):

High blood pressure ‚Äî what to ask a doctor When your heart pumps blood through arteries, the pressure of blood against the walls of arteries is called blood pressure. Blood pressure is measured by two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Systolic blood pressure is the highest pressure during the cycle of heart contractions. Diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When blood pressure becomes too high, this creates additional load on the heart and blood vessels. If blood pressure remains high constantly, you have an increased risk of infarcts and other vascular diseases, strokes, kidney diseases and other health problems. Below are questions that you can ask your medical worker to help control blood pressure. Questions How can I change my lifestyle to lower blood pressure? What is a diet, healthy for the heart? Is it possible sometimes to eat something that is not healthy for the heart? How can one eat properly when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit the amount of salt? Are there other spices that I can use to make food tasty? Is it possible to drink alcohol? What amount is permissible? What can I do to quit smoking? Is it possible to be near people who smoke? Should I measure blood pressure at home? What equipment should I buy? Where can I learn to use it? How often do I need to measure blood pressure? Should I record the readings and bring them to the next appointment? If I cannot measure blood pressure myself, where else can I measure it? What should my blood pressure readings be? Should I rest before measuring blood pressure? When should I contact a medical worker? What is the level of my cholesterol? Do I need to take medications for its lowering? Is it possible for me to have a sexual life? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn) or avanafil (Stendra) for problems with erection? What medications am I taking for treatment of high blood pressure? Do they have side effects? What should I ...

Original English:

Type 2 diabetes - what to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in your blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage due to diabetes, and make your life better. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your diabetes. Questions Ask your provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulses in your feet. Also ask these questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I do when I check them? What problems should I call my provider about? Who should trim my toenails? Is it OK if I trim them? How should I take care of my feet every day? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? Should I see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your provider about getting exercise, including: Before I start, do I need to have my heart checked? My eyes? My feet? What type of exercise program should I do? What type of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I bring with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should I next have an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact my eye doctor about? Ask your provider about meeting with a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: What foods increase my blood sugar the most? What foods can help me with my weight loss goals? Ask your provider about your diabetes medicines: When should I take them? What should I do if I miss a dose? Are there any side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar level at home? Should I do it at different times of the day? What is too low? What is too high? What should I do if my blood sugar is too low or too high? Should I get a medical alert bracelet or necklace? Sugar diabetes type 2 ‚Äî what to ask the doctor Sugar diabetes type 2 after diagnosis is a chronic disease, which causes high level of sugar (glucose) in the blood. This can damage your organs. Also this can lead to heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps for control of symptoms, prevention of complications of diabetes and improvement of quality of life. Below are questions, which you can ask your doctor, to help yourself in care for diabetes. Questions Ask the doctor to check nerves, skin and pulse on your feet. Also ask these questions: ‚Ä¢ How often do I need to check feet? What should I do when I check them? About what problems should I inform the doctor? ‚Ä¢ Who should cut my toenails? Can I cut them myself? ‚Ä¢ How should I care for feet every day? What shoes and socks should I wear? ‚Ä¢ Do I need to see a doctor for feet (podiatrist)? Ask the doctor about physical activity exercises, including: ‚Ä¢ Before I start, do I need to check heart? Eyes? Feet? ‚Ä¢ What program of physical exercises should I perform? What types of activity should I avoid? ‚Ä¢ When should I check blood sugar level during exercises? What should I take with me when I do physical activity? Should I eat before or during exercises? Do I need to adjust medication dosage when I do physical activity? When do I next need to check eyes at an oculist (optometrist or ophthalmologist)? About what vision problems should I inform the oculist? Ask the doctor about meeting with a dietitian. Questions to a dietitian may include: ‚Ä¢ What foods raise blood sugar level the most? ‚Ä¢ What foods can help me in achieving goals for weight loss? Ask the doctor about your medications from diabetes: ‚Ä¢ When should I take them? ‚Ä¢ What should I do if I missed a dose? ‚Ä¢ Are there side effects? How often should I check blood sugar level at home? Should I do this at different times of day? What is considered too low? What is considered too high? Item: EVAL_0333 | Document: DIAB_002 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Low blood sugar level ‚Äî self-help Low blood sugar level is a condition that occurs when the blood sugar level (glucose) is below normal. Low blood sugar level can occur in people with diabetes mellitus who take insulin or some other medications for diabetes control. Low blood sugar level can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn to recognize symptoms of low blood sugar level and prevent them. What is low blood sugar level? Low blood sugar level is called hypoglycemia. Blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is considered low and can harm you. Blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a reason for immediate actions. You are in a risk group for developing low blood sugar level if you have diabetes mellitus and you take any of the following diabetes medications: - Insulin - Glibenclamide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin) or nateglinide (Starlix) Also you have an increased risk of developing low blood sugar level if you have already had previous cases of low blood sugar level. Recognizing low blood sugar level Learn how to determine when blood sugar level is decreasing. Symptoms include: - Weakness or fatigue - Trembling - Sweating - Headache - Feeling of hunger - Anxiety, nervousness or anxiousness - Irritability - Difficulty of clear thinking - Double vision in the eyes or blurred vision - Frequent or strong heartbeat Sometimes blood sugar level can be too low even if you have no symptoms. If it becomes too low, you can: - Lose consciousness - Experience seizures - Fall into a coma Some people who suffer from diabetes mellitus for a long time stop feeling the decrease of blood sugar level. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your medical worker if wearing a continuous glucose monitor with a sensor can help you detect when blood sugar level becomes too low to help prevent symptoms. Check blood sugar level frequently Talk with your medical worker about when you should check blood sugar level eve...
